{"uri": "8201704571", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:39:07", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:39:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:38:54Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9647058844566345, "url": "https://roanoke.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_568b3a27-4ff8-50ae-891e-fc7cd8e1a06e.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news {"uri": "2024-06-404784049", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-28", "time": "12:24:58", "dateTime": "2024-06-28T12:24:58Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-28T11:00:06Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9490196108818054, "url": "https://www.register-herald.com/news/nation_world/ever-feel-exhausted-by-swiping-through-dating-apps-you-might-be-experiencing-burnout/article_12c6db51-f055-576d-8f02-36fba770c9d8.html", "title": "Ever feel exhausted by swiping through dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "The dating app Tinder is shown on a smartphone on Wednesday, June 26, 2024, in New York. Plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app. But many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting.\n\nNEW YORK (AP) -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nFor Jennifer Stavros, a freelance journalist in Los Angeles, her time in the online dating world has \"been a mixed bag.\" While she's still giving platforms like Tinder, Hinge and OkCupid a try, Stavros notes she's experienced a recent cycle of matches that don't go far.\n\n\"I have a conversation ... and it'll go okay. (But) then it will just drop, or it'll just hit a wall somewhere,\" Stavros, 42, said. \"It's not making me feel super hopeful.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way, leading them to log off and decide that \"dating is important, but my security, my self (worth) is more important.\"\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments. A Hinge spokesperson says this feature is aimed at helping vulnerable groups -- particularly women, people of color and LGBTQ+ individuals -- avoid unwanted interactions based on personal preferences.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said in an interview. Users are also able to \"surf the grid\" on incognito mode, which is typically a paid feature, for free in some locations, he added.\n\n\"All Grindr users are under some form of challenges in their lives,\" Arison said. \"Our job has always been to create a safe environment for people to be who they are.\"\n\nWhen asked about dating app burnout overall, Arison said \"we've not seen any fatigue of Grindr users\" but he noted there's growing hunger for innovation.\n\nThat's evidenced by the scores of updates that have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\n\"We are just scratching the tip of the iceberg,\" said Anindya Ghose, Heinz Riehl Chair Professor of technology and marketing at New York University's Stern School of Business, who believes AI could help alleviate burnout but transparency will be key.\n\nSuch innovations may be a way to keep people hooked on dating apps. Espitia is among those who say she'd be open to seeing platforms implement further updates -- including the use of AI -- if it helps improve connections with people around her.\n\n\"We're in this new age of finding love,\" she said. \"People really are like starved for love -- and I think if that (technology) can help, why not?\"", "source": {"uri": "register-herald.com", "dataType": "news", {"uri": "8201152364", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "06:58:03", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T06:58:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T06:57:42Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9450980424880981, "url": "https://www.manilatimes.net/2024/06/29/entertainment-lifestyle/ever-feel-exhausted-by-swiping-through-dating-apps-you-might-be-experiencing-burnout/1954074", "title": "Ever feel exhausted by swiping through dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nGet the latest news\n\ndelivered to your inbox Sign up for The Manila Times newsletters By signing up with an email address, I acknowledge that I have read and agree to the Terms of Service and Privacy Policy.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nAdvertisement\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nFor Jennifer Stavros, a freelance journalist in Los Angeles, her time in the online dating world has \"been a mixed bag.\" While she's still giving platforms like Tinder, Hinge and OkCupid a try, Stavros notes she's experienced a recent cycle of matches that don't go far.\n\n\"I have a conversation... and it'll go okay. (But) then it will just drop, or it'll just hit a wall somewhere,\" Stavros, 42, said. \"It's not making me feel super hopeful.\"\n\nAdvertisement\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way, leading them to log off and decide that \"dating is important, but my security, my self (worth) is more important.\"\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments. A Hinge spokesperson says this feature is aimed at helping vulnerable groups -- particularly women, people of color and LGBTQ+ individuals -- avoid unwanted interactions based on personal preferences.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said in an interview. Users are also able to \"surf the grid\" on incognito mode, which is typically a paid feature, for free in some locations, he added.\n\n\"All Grindr users are under some form of challenges in their lives,\" Arison said. \"Our job has always been to create a safe environment for people to be who they are.\"\n\nAdvertisement\n\nWhen asked about dating app burnout overall, Arison said \"we've not seen any fatigue of Grindr users\" but he noted there's growing hunger for innovation.\n\nThat's evidenced by the scores of updates that have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\n\"We are just scratching the tip of the iceberg,\" said Anindya Ghose, Heinz Riehl Chair Professor of technology and marketing at New York University's Stern School of Business, who believes AI could help alleviate burnout but transparency will be key.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nSuch innovations may be a way to keep people hooked on dating apps. Espitia is among those who say she'd be open to seeing platforms implement further updates -- including the use of AI -- if it helps improve connections with people around her.\n\n\"We're in this new age of finding love,\" she said. \"People really are like starved for love -- and I think if that (technology) can help, why not?\"", "source": {"uri": "manilatimes.net", "dataType": "news", "title": {"uri": "2024-06-405053363", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-28", "time": "16:34:50", "dateTime": "2024-06-28T16:34:50Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-28T13:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9372549057006836, "url": "https://www.keremeosreview.com/trending-now/app-exhaustion-downs-of-dating-online-leading-to-burnout-for-some-singles-7417434", "title": "App exhaustion: downs of dating online leading to burnout for some singles", "body": "'It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little'\n\nWhile plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nFor Jennifer Stavros, a freelance journalist in Los Angeles, her time in the online dating world has \"been a mixed bag.\" While she's still giving platforms like Tinder, Hinge and OkCupid a try, Stavros notes she's experienced a recent cycle of matches that don't go far.\n\n\"I have a conversation ... and it'll go okay. (But) then it will just drop, or it'll just hit a wall somewhere,\" Stavros, 42, said. \"It's not making me feel super hopeful.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way, leading them to log off and decide that \"dating is important, but my security, my self (worth) is more important.\"\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments. A Hinge spokesperson says this feature is aimed at helping vulnerable groups -- particularly women, people of color and LGBTQ+ individuals -- avoid unwanted interactions based on personal preferences.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said in an interview. Users are also able to \"surf the grid\" on incognito mode, which is typically a paid feature, for free in some locations, he added.\n\n\"All Grindr users are under some form of challenges in their lives,\" Arison said. \"Our job has always been to create a safe environment for people to be who they are.\"\n\nWhen asked about dating app burnout overall, Arison said \"we've not seen any fatigue of Grindr users\" but he noted there's growing hunger for innovation.\n\nThat's evidenced by the scores of updates that have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\n\"We are just scratching the tip of the iceberg,\" said Anindya Ghose, Heinz Riehl Chair Professor of technology and marketing at New York University's Stern School of Business, who believes AI could help alleviate burnout but transparency will be key.\n\nSuch innovations may be a way to keep people hooked on dating apps. Espitia is among those who say she'd be open to seeing platforms implement further updates -- including the use of AI -- if it helps improve connections with people around her.\n\n\"We're in this new age of finding love,\" she said. \"People really are like starved for love -- and I think if that (technology) can help, why not?\"\n\nREAD ALSO: First comes Tinder, then marriage: UBCO prof examines You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? Survey participants say they are most likely to cheat at a party Over half of respondents (54.5%) believe a relationship can recover from cheating Unfaithful partners are most likely to cheat with an {"uri": "2024-06-404750970", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-28", "time": "11:56:10", "dateTime": "2024-06-28T11:56:10Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-27T23:23:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9176470637321472, "url": "https://www.usnews.com/news/best-states/new-york/articles/2024-06-28/ever-feel-exhausted-by-swiping-through-dating-apps-you-might-be-experiencing-burnout", "title": "Ever Feel Exhausted by Swiping Through Dating Apps? You Might Be Experiencing Burnout", "body": "NEW YORK (AP) -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nFor Jennifer Stavros, a freelance journalist in Los Angeles, her time in the online dating world has \"been a mixed bag.\" While she's still giving platforms like Tinder, Hinge and OkCupid a try, Stavros notes she's experienced a recent cycle of matches that don't go far.\n\n\"I have a conversation ... and it'll go okay. (But) then it will just drop, or it'll just hit a wall somewhere,\" Stavros, 42, said. \"It's not making me feel super hopeful.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way, leading them to log off and decide that \"dating is important, but my security, my self (worth) is more important.\"\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments. A Hinge spokesperson says this feature is aimed at helping vulnerable groups -- particularly women, people of color and LGBTQ+ individuals -- avoid unwanted interactions based on personal preferences.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said in an interview. Users are also able to \"surf the grid\" on incognito mode, which is typically a paid feature, for free in some locations, he added.\n\n\"All Grindr users are under some form of challenges in their lives,\" Arison said. \"Our job has always been to create a safe environment for people to be who they are.\"\n\nWhen asked about dating app burnout overall, Arison said \"we've not seen any fatigue of Grindr users\" but he noted there's growing hunger for innovation.\n\nThat's evidenced by the scores of updates that have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\n\"We are just scratching the tip of the iceberg,\" said Anindya Ghose, Heinz Riehl Chair Professor of technology and marketing at New York University's Stern School of Business, who believes AI could help alleviate burnout but transparency will be key.\n\nSuch innovations may be a way to keep people hooked on dating apps. Espitia is among those who say she'd be open to seeing platforms implement further updates -- including the use of AI -- if it helps improve connections with people around her.\n\n\"We're in this new age of finding love,\" she said. \"People really are like starved for love -- and I think if that (technology) can help, why not?\"\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "A recent Eventbrite report highlights a significant trend among young singles, particularly Gen Z and millennials, who are turning away from dating apps in favor of in-person events. This shift is attributed to a growing dissatisfaction with the superficial nature of online conversations, sa...\n\nNEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nJohn Deere announces 600+ layoffs at East Moline, Davenport and Dubuque Sarah Goodwin says her husband's life was 'swirling out of control' before he was killed Quad-Cities prepare for 18-foot Mississippi River flood Davenport School Board votes to end lease agreement with North Family YMCA August rap show at The Mark to feature Bone Thugs-N-Harmony, Xzibit and Yung Joc Rock Island's Dirty Shirley closing permanently next week Man accused of hanging his dog by its leash and punching the animal PV standout and Cross-Fit athlete named 2024 Beat the Elite Runners Brian Johnson Feds fine Iowa restaurants amid child labor law dispute Judge issues summary judgment in favor of Davenport police officer and city in wrongful death suit Two-time national champion Will Reemtsma to compete against the best at U.S. Olympic Team Trials Lori A. Kissell Red, White and Boom! postponed, new date pending Rock Island-Milan schools to get weapons detectors, other security updates\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity {"uri": "8201706977", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:41:52", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:41:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:41:38Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_a096e813-392a-5d1b-915f-bcd7c7c288ca.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... Marion's Spillway Bridge & Co. set to reopen under new owners Man dies after jumping from pickup truck in Glen Alpine; driver charged Marion clothing company starts nonprofit for family events, services McDowell Sheriff's Office searching for missing woman McDowell man on probation charged with meth possession Marion resident shares concerns about city rate increases Marion man faces drug charges following search of home Asheville man charged with robbery in McDowell Historic Carson House breaks ground on $1.2 million visitor center West Marion project to revamp former school gets technical, fundraising aid Marion man charged with fentanyl possession McDowell County gets money to celebrate U.S. 250th anniversary Letter: The Republican plan to rig the 2020 election Hickory man sentenced to 11 years for child porn; Police found 50,000 images on devices Adventures with Dandy: Reporter, wife, dog enjoy Marion Wing Factory\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? Unfaithful partners are most likely to cheat with an ex partner Survey participants say they are most likely to cheat at a party Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Over {"uri": "8201711766", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:46:32", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:46:32Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:46:21Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://missoulian.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_e11c3460-7c00-5e49-aad4-9b5d536d6868.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... 'Devastating': Montana's Flathead cherry crop wiped out by January Arctic freeze Missoula City Council approves new urban camping laws UPDATED: Pennsylvania woman killed in East Glacier identified 'Armageddon' for Montana Hi-Line farms without Milk River fix Big changes planned for deadly Hwy 93 stretch between Missoula and Florence Missoula ranked 15th best-run city in the US Whitefish physician accused of large Medicare fraud scheme Business Buzz: New eatery/bar, new acai bowl cafe, new flights from Missoula Detectives investigating after pride flags vandalized in Missoula South Hills subdivision plans approved Closure looms for Child Development Center serving children with disabilities in Montana Lake County crash kills 17-year-old Man arrested in fatal shooting of woman in Helena Back-to-back accidents cause delays on Going-to-the-Sun Road on Wednesday St. Pat's, Community Medical Center to build new inpatient rehab hospital in Missoula\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Participants offer various excuses for cheating What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? {"uri": "8201703849", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:38:48", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:38:48Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:38:24Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8980392217636108, "url": "https://pressofatlanticcity.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_1828c736-ce3c-5eef-9885-a25837234a13.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... Atlantic City Beer and Music Festival canceled North Wildwood police located man in crisis they were searching for Absecon man killed in Galloway motorcycle accident New Jersey shut down the popular Remedee Coffee in Atlantic City CRDA set to reject Atlantic Avenue road diet Cape May lifeguards mourn longtime leader Harry 'Buzz' Mogck Jr. Two arrested in undercover theft operation on Atlantic City boardwalk Powerboats bring the noise and the thrills to Atlantic City Pleasantville wide receiver/defensive back commits to Penn State Jason Kelce's 4th annual bartending fundraiser set for Wednesday in Sea Isle City Summer bike hours enforced on Atlantic City Boardwalk Hearing Tuesday on offshore wind farm plan to run transmission cables under Atlantic City CRDA says no to road diet, despite city's plea Chicken Guy restaurant debuts Tuesday in Atlantic City Postal worker injured following truck fire in Margate\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? Survey participants say they are most likely to cheat at a party Over half of respondents (54.5%) believe a relationship can recover from cheating Unfaithful partners are most likely to cheat with an Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading...
