North Platte Planning Commission agenda Polk honored, pitches gem and offense thrives in FNBO rout of Kearney\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? suspect dead, State Patrol says Lincoln woman wins $220K in Nebraska Pick 5; other $22K winning ticket still unclaimed City Hall: It's a \"bring your own\" Lincoln's Uncle Sam Jam this year Nebraska volleyball's Harper Murray pleads no contest to DUI Garner Industries relocates in Lincoln after tornado destroys plant Rehab, reset: Jordy Bahl readying for second take with Nebraska softball Ex-Lincoln officer alleges city fired him for raising concerns of misconduct at LPD Former Nebraska lawmaker Adam Morfeld to leave nonprofit he helped found Why Nebraska legend Jordan Larson came out of retirement to play in fourth Olympics 'It's divisive:' Handful of Tesla Cybertrucks cruising around Lincoln Recruiting: Nebraska adds JUCO wide receiver, offensive line target commits to Oregon State Lincoln Airport's proposed budget calls for a significant property tax increase Winning lottery ticket worth $22,000 sold in Lincoln\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? {"uri": "8199951989", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-28", "time": "11:29:48", "dateTime": "2024-06-28T11:29:48Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-28T11:29:36Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8901960849761963, "url": "https://www.thepeterboroughexaminer.com/life/ever-feel-exhausted-by-swiping-through-dating-apps-you-might-be-experiencing-burnout/article_10996e39-7169-5c81-a5f2-9d242784daad.html", "title": "Ever feel exhausted by swiping through dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK (AP) -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\n\u00d7\n\nAlready a Subscriber? Sign in You are logged in Switch accounts\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nFor Jennifer Stavros, a freelance journalist in Los Angeles, her time in the online dating world has \"been a mixed bag.\" While she's still giving platforms like Tinder, Hinge and OkCupid a try, Stavros notes she's experienced a recent cycle of matches that don't go far.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\n\"I have a conversation ... and it'll go okay. (But) then it will just drop, or it'll just hit a wall somewhere,\" Stavros, 42, said. \"It's not making me feel super hopeful.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way, leading them to log off and decide that \"dating is important, but my security, my self (worth) is more important.\"\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments. A Hinge spokesperson says this feature is aimed at helping vulnerable groups -- particularly women, people of color and LGBTQ+ individuals -- avoid unwanted interactions based on personal preferences.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said in an interview. Users are also able to \"surf the grid\" on incognito mode, which is typically a paid feature, for free in some locations, he added.\n\n\"All Grindr users are under some form of challenges in their lives,\" Arison said. \"Our job has always been to create a safe environment for people to be who they are.\"\n\nWhen asked about dating app burnout overall, Arison said \"we've not seen any fatigue of Grindr users\" but he noted there's growing hunger for innovation.\n\nThat's evidenced by the scores of updates that have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\n\"We are just scratching the tip of the iceberg,\" said Anindya Ghose, Heinz Riehl Chair Professor of technology and marketing at New York University's Stern School of Business, who believes AI could help alleviate burnout but transparency will be key.\n\nSuch innovations may be a way to keep people hooked on dating apps. Espitia is among those who say she'd be open to seeing platforms implement further updates -- including the use of AI -- if it helps improve connections with people around her.\n\n\"We're in this new age of finding love,\" she said. \"People really are like starved for love -- and I think if that (technology) can help, why not?\"", "source": {"uri": "thepeterboroughexaminer.com", "dataType": {"uri": "8201711514", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:46:25", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:46:25Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:46:02Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8823529481887817, "url": "https://statesville.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_f23dd307-eab8-5255-a437-189553da47d8.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... Statesville planning board OKs development with at least 900 homes Two Rowan-Iredell firefighters killed in West Virginia ATV crash Bans on gas-powered leaf blowers are growing. So is blowback from the landscaping industry Novant ends takeover bid for Iredell hospitals. Future of Lake Norman, Davis up in air Statesville motorcyclist killed in crash near Mooresville Wednesday New West Iredell head football coach: 'Your brand is what you make it' US Marshals nab Statesville man wanted in fatal April shooting Greenville man struck, killed by tractor-trailer in Iredell County Mooresville man convicted of murder at 14 suing state official over denied parole Out of Our Past: Television comes to Statesville; Troutman postmaster retires 'I was terrified,' accuser testifies in rape trial of Illinois basketball star Terrence Shannon Jr. Juneteenth Celebration in Statesville: A reminder of how far we've come, how far to go Fontanez takes reins as Lake Norman baseball coach Statesville woman found dead on Hickory Avenue; death considered homicide Mooresville man sentenced to 9 years for possession of firearm by felon\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? {"uri": "8201706002", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:40:41", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:40:41Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:40:24Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8745098114013672, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_7ca2ee80-c78e-50d3-a8df-d3e307ee31db.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... St. Helena's V. Sattui Winery sued by former employee alleging wage abuse Live music at Friday Nights in the Park returns to downtown Napa Supervisor Belia Ramos files court documents alleging harassment Alta Heights residents sue city of Napa for approving day care in neighborhood Treasury Wine Estates hit with Napa lawsuit claiming labor violations Battle of the Bands coming to downtown Napa Manslaughter case over cyclist couple killed in Napa moves forward Update: Forward progress stopped on fire off Jameson Canyon Road Former American Canyon school principal charged after ex-teacher's sex abuse arrest Protesters disrupt Tyler Florence visit to inaugural Napa Cookbook Fest Update: Napa motorcyclist who died in Highway 29 collision identified Napa parents: Are Napa middle schools safe? Newsom attacks 'delusional California bashers' in unorthodox speech Napa Police: Man arrested after sexual assault Napa Town & Country Fair announces musicians, opens ticket sales\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Over half of survey participants have been the victims of cheating Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? {"uri": "8201705816", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-29", "time": "15:40:31", "dateTime": "2024-06-29T15:40:31Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-29T15:40:10Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8235294222831726, "url": "https://lacrossetribune.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/technology/tinder-hinge-grindr-dating-app-burnout/article_0bfe8485-99e8-5972-824a-308001037a72.html", "title": "Swiped out: Exhausted by dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "NEW YORK -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... Steve Cahalan: New cafe to open in Melrose, Onalaska bakery relocates and La Crosse ice cream shop set to close Mississippi River levels in La Crosse expected to peak at moderate flood stage next week as more rain in forecast Second person charged in trafficking of teen girl in La Crosse area New River Point District agreement leaves only one lot unclaimed for $300M La Crosse development Riverfest, campers impacted by high water as Mississippi River rises in La Crosse and Winona Police arrest 13-year-old tied to one of two recent La Crosse shooting incidents Packers name next president/CEO More restrictions on homeless camping proposed by La Crosse mayor, city council members Warrant issued for Iowa man accused of assaulting nurse Former Moldovan parliament member, Olympic weightlifter accused of installing illegal ATM readers in La Crosse La Crosse man charged with injury hit-and-run after three-vehicle crash Witness: Rural Melrose man shot Kyle Dahl in the head after Dahl drove slowly past parked car Wisconsin Supreme Court justices 'shocked,' call for probe into leaked court order on abortion lawsuit Steve Cahalan: Tasty Thai to replace Il Luigi Italian Cuisine restaurant in Onalaska Man arrested for killing woman in La Crosse County was found not guilty of violent 2009 attack\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's A. B. Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way.\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said.\n\nScores of updates have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\nTexting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Texting ex-partners, flirting online, staying on dating apps: Survey reveals most common infidelity habits -- and why cheaters do them Participants offer various excuses for cheating Nearly one in five respondents signed up for a dating app while in a relationship What behavior is acceptable to check a partner's fidelity? Unfaithful partners are most likely to cheat with an ex partner Survey participants say they are most likely to cheat at a party Over half of respondents (54.5%) believe a relationship can recover {"uri": "8199944753", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-28", "time": "11:25:01", "dateTime": "2024-06-28T11:25:01Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-28T11:24:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8156862854957581, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/news/state-regional/ever-feel-exhausted-by-swiping-through-dating-apps-you-might-be-experiencing-burnout/article_8510d695-a13c-54fa-bc57-ab5a24ea9890.html", "title": "Ever feel exhausted by swiping through dating apps? You might be experiencing burnout", "body": "By WYATTE GRANTHAM-PHILIPS - AP Business Writer\n\nNEW YORK (AP) -- While plenty of happy couples can trace their meet-cute moment to an online dating app, many others find the never-ending process of likes, swipes, taps and awkward DMs that go nowhere to be exhausting -- leading to a phenomenon known as \"dating app burnout.\"\n\nThat was the case for Marilyn Espitia, a 31-year-old freelance photo editor and photographer in California who first ventured into online dating in college, when she met her former partner and now father of her child on OkCupid.\n\nToday she is single, and has been for about three years. While she's still a \"hopeless romantic\" who plans to keep using these platforms -- primarily Hinge -- Espitia says she'll get off an app or pause her profile when it becomes a little too much.\n\n\"It starts getting overwhelming,\" Espitia said.\n\nLicensed clinical psychologist Yasmine Saad says that about 3 out of every 4 people she works with use dating apps, and anywhere between 80 to 90% have expressed feeling similar fatigue or burnout as Espitia at some point.\n\nPeople are also reading... Update: Forward progress stopped on fire off Jameson Canyon Road St. Helena's V. Sattui Winery sued by former employee alleging wage abuse Live music at Friday Nights in the Park returns to downtown Napa Alta Heights residents sue city of Napa for approving day care in neighborhood Update: Napa motorcyclist who died in Highway 29 collision identified Manslaughter case over cyclist couple killed in Napa moves forward Battle of the Bands coming to downtown Napa Protesters disrupt Tyler Florence visit to inaugural Napa Cookbook Fest Napa Police: Man arrested after sexual assault Napa parents: Are Napa middle schools safe? Former American Canyon school principal charged after ex-teacher's sex abuse arrest Napa Town & Country Fair announces musicians, opens ticket sales Newsom attacks 'delusional California bashers' in unorthodox speech New bike lane sweeper helps keep Napa cyclists, drivers safe Felony arson charges dropped against Napa man; probation ordered\n\nThat's due in part because success is never promised with online dating, regardless of whether you're looking for a lifelong partner or casual fling.\n\n\"It's a very difficult process for people because you invest a lot, then you receive little,\" said Saad, founder and CEO of Madison Park Psychological Services in New York. \"It triggers a lot of hopelessness and a lot of self-esteem issues.\"\n\nKathryn Coduto, an assistant professor of media science at Boston University who has been studying online dating since 2016, says dating app burnout is probably as old as the apps themselves, noting that people had experienced fatigue with earlier desktop-dominant platforms like eHarmony or Match.com as well.\n\nBut these days, burnout may be intensified by the fact there's an app for just about every part of our daily lives, and that constant connectivity can be too much. Pandemic-era \"Zoom fatigue\" has spilled over into other areas of tech consumption, Coduto said, and online dating isn't immune.\n\nThat doesn't mean dating apps are going away anytime soon. Research shows usage has remained relatively stable over recent years.\n\nPew Research Center said that 3 out of 10 U.S. adults reported ever using an online dating site or app as of July 2022 -- identical to the share found in October 2019, months before COVID-19 impacted much of daily life, including dating habits.\n\nWhile there was some uptick in new user downloads at the start of the pandemic, Coduto's research found more of a spike in usage from those who already had dating apps and were spending more time on them during lockdowns. But those same lockdowns also limited in-person interactions, and the ripple effects are still being felt today.\n\n\"The pandemic increased loneliness,\" Saad said. \"But it also boosted the hopelessness ... because even the apps were not meeting the needs of people for socialization.\"\n\nFor Jennifer Stavros, a freelance journalist in Los Angeles, her time in the online dating world has \"been a mixed bag.\" While she's still giving platforms like Tinder, Hinge and OkCupid a try, Stavros notes she's experienced a recent cycle of matches that don't go far.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"I have a conversation ... and it'll go okay. (But) then it will just drop, or it'll just hit a wall somewhere,\" Stavros, 42, said. \"It's not making me feel super hopeful.\"\n\nOthers add that it can also become easy to forget there are people on the other side of those swipes and likes, making them feel dismissed while looking for connections.\n\n\"I think that sense of swiping endlessly absolutely plays into burnout,\" Coduto said. \"You're treating people like a card deck because that's what you're looking at.\"\n\nYumei He, an assistant professor of management science at Tulane University's Freeman School of Business who has also been studying online dating, said that hurtful experiences -- such as being ghosted -- can cause users to not trust a platform, or assume all future interactions there will end up the same way, leading them to log off and decide that \"dating is important, but my security, my self (worth) is more important.\"\n\nAnd of course, burnout doesn't look the same for everyone. Experiences can range widely depending on gender, sexuality, race and ethnicity. Researchers have found that women and genderqueer individuals, for example, are more likely to face harassment than men, while racial and ethnic minorities are often fetishized in online dating spaces, or experience other discrimination resulting from sexual racism.\n\nThe trauma of experiencing discrimination and other abuse on a dating app can make it very difficult to stay on a platform or trust it again, Coduto said.\n\nCompanies are increasingly navigating ways to address all of this. Hinge, for example, in April launched \"Hidden Words,\" which allows its users to filter out words, phrases and emojis in their incoming likes and comments. A Hinge spokesperson says this feature is aimed at helping vulnerable groups -- particularly women, people of color and LGBTQ+ individuals -- avoid unwanted interactions based on personal preferences.\n\nGay dating and social networking app Grindr alerts users of potential safety threats in their area, which has been particularly critical for LGBTQ+ people in countries who may face police raids and other dangers, CEO George Arison said in an interview. Users are also able to \"surf the grid\" on incognito mode, which is typically a paid feature, for free in some locations, he added.\n\n\"All Grindr users are under some form of challenges in their lives,\" Arison said. \"Our job has always been to create a safe environment for people to be who they are.\"\n\nWhen asked about dating app burnout overall, Arison said \"we've not seen any fatigue of Grindr users\" but he noted there's growing hunger for innovation.\n\nThat's evidenced by the scores of updates that have recently emerged across various dating apps -- from a new prompts option on Bumble, which shifts how the platform historically facilitated its \"first move,\" to Tinder's \"Matchmaker\" feature allowing friends to recommend profiles for each other and Hinge's tests of \"your turn limits\" to help fend off ghosting.\n\nA handful of popular platforms, including Grindr and Tinder, say they've started integrating artificial intelligence to help identify potential harmful messages and other safety precautions. Some are also looking at AI possibilities such as using the technology to strengthen matching algorithms or offer users' message prompts and date ideas.\n\n\"We are just scratching the tip of the iceberg,\" said Anindya Ghose, Heinz Riehl Chair Professor of technology and marketing at New York University's Stern School of Business, who believes AI could help alleviate burnout but transparency will be key.\n\nSuch innovations may be a way to keep people hooked on dating apps. Espitia is among those who say she'd be open to seeing platforms implement further updates -- including the use of AI -- if it helps improve connections with people around her.\n\n\"We're in this new age of finding love,\" she said. \"People really are like starved for love -- and I think if that (technology) can help, why not?\"\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press.
